<div id="review_23884264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23884264" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/236411-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291695844p2/236411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23884264?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/236411-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23884264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4633281126575302857">Despite the title, you don’t start out thinking of Jim Dixon as particularly lucky. He was low man on the totem pole at a provincial English university where the one on top, Professor Welch, was a quirky twit of a man —- absent-minded and egocentric with an excess of class prerogative. Jim was not so lucky in love either. The woman he was with, a fellow academic, plied whatever feminine wiles were available to one with a rather plain appearance. Christine, the more striking young lady Jim met an</span>
  <span id="freeText4633281126575302857" style="display:none">Despite the title, you don’t start out thinking of Jim Dixon as particularly lucky. He was low man on the totem pole at a provincial English university where the one on top, Professor Welch, was a quirky twit of a man —- absent-minded and egocentric with an excess of class prerogative. Jim was not so lucky in love either. The woman he was with, a fellow academic, plied whatever feminine wiles were available to one with a rather plain appearance. Christine, the more striking young lady Jim met and liked, was spoken for by Professor Welch’s son, a pretentious snob and complete lout. Jim himself was not dashing, he lacked ambition, and he had a penchant for the bottle that could get him in trouble. He was likable, though. Amis blessed him with a keen, sardonic wit and an ability to see through the BS. Jim would be a great model for a Judd Apatow movie: the slacker with a brain trying to get what he wants without stepping on any but the most deserving toes. I suspect most readers, like me, end up wanting to see the residue of Jim’s designs pay off.<br /><br />The book was funny and well-written, but was also good for showing a different slice of life. It was published in 1954, an era when protocols were more proper and snide remarks were often just unspoken asides. At the same time, a certain political incorrectness seemed <i>de rigueur</i>, especially regarding women. This was also a period when the English equivalent to the G.I. Bill was in place and Amis knew from his own experiences as a university lecturer that cultural dynamics were changing. <i>Lucky Jim</i> was viewed as a victory for the common man. The snobatorium, with all its stultifying disdain for the masses, was given a right good comeuppance. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4633281126575302857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_195561725" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/195561725" />
    <a title="Samuel Williams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5884696-samuel-williams"><img alt="Samuel Williams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456875123p2/5884696.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/195561725?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samuel Williams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samuel Williams" href="/user/show/5884696-samuel-williams">Samuel Williams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182599" href="/book/show/95287.Lucky_Jim">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer195561725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9694562208361794490">Perhaps I'm a stuffed-shirted bore, but I didn't find <i>Lucky Jim</i> anywhere near as funny as it was made out to be. Granted, it did make me smile sometimes, and laugh out loud occasionally. But it doesn't seem to have much else going for it. There's wit enough, but much of the comedy is physical rather than verbal, with strong elements of farce, and would probably work better on stage or screen than in print. The language is gratingly formal and often feels mechanical, even when viewed as a parody </span>
  <span id="freeText9694562208361794490" style="display:none">Perhaps I'm a stuffed-shirted bore, but I didn't find <i>Lucky Jim</i> anywhere near as funny as it was made out to be. Granted, it did make me smile sometimes, and laugh out loud occasionally. But it doesn't seem to have much else going for it. There's wit enough, but much of the comedy is physical rather than verbal, with strong elements of farce, and would probably work better on stage or screen than in print. The language is gratingly formal and often feels mechanical, even when viewed as a parody of academic writing.<br /><br />Kingsley's character portraits are perceptive, but somehow failed to grab me. At the heart of it all is Jim Dixon, a supposedly loveable slacker who expects luck to carry him through life. And – moral of the story – it does. Jim comes to the realisation that 'It was luck you needed all along; with just a little more luck he'd have been able to switch his life onto a momentarily adjoining track'. With more than a little luck, Jim gets the girl of his dreams, whose pleasant personality is a direct result of her fortunate good looks, and escapes from Margaret, whose histrionic personality is a direct result of her less-than-fortunate looks. Dumping Margaret is a perfectly acceptable course of action – it's nothing personal; she's just unlucky.<br /><br />To the average mid-twenties male who wishes that the women in his life would stop being so damned complicated and just sleep with him already, it may be a soothing balm. But for someone who stopped laughing along with Jim a hundred pages ago, it's plainly ridiculous and irritatingly shallow. The last thing Jim needs is more luck – what he really needs is to pull his bloody socks up, control his pathological face-pulling and stop looking for the quick-fix solution to every problem that comes his way. Oddly enough, the character with whom I most sympathise is just another obstacle in Dixon's path: Michie, the student who actually wants to get something out of his university education. Fortunately, my lecturers are much more capable than Dixon and Welch, who, to me, seem just as bad as each other.<br /><br />Perhaps I'm being a little harsh, and taking an overly serious approach to what should be a lighthearted campus romp. But when the laughs don't come thick and fast enough, the superficiality of this novel really stands out, and I can't help but feel as if I'm reading the 1950s equivalent of a National Lampoon film.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9694562208361794490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1502503178?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Ehlers" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers">Julie Ehlers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175750" href="/book/show/13331149-lucky-jim">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2120450-julie-ehlers?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2120450-julie-ehlers?shelf=funny">funny</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1502503178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12325617955368940474">Many years ago, I briefly dated a guy whose favorite book was <i>Lucky Jim</i>. I'd barely heard of the novel at the time, but I made a mental note of it, and for whatever reason I've now finally gotten around to reading it. I wish I'd read it back when I was dating him, because this portrayal of a totally clueless dude who sometimes hurts people but is completely astonished to realize he's done so because he sees himself as a pure and honest soul just fumbling around would have given me quite an insig</span>
  <span id="freeText12325617955368940474" style="display:none">Many years ago, I briefly dated a guy whose favorite book was <i>Lucky Jim</i>. I'd barely heard of the novel at the time, but I made a mental note of it, and for whatever reason I've now finally gotten around to reading it. I wish I'd read it back when I was dating him, because this portrayal of a totally clueless dude who sometimes hurts people but is completely astonished to realize he's done so because he sees himself as a pure and honest soul just fumbling around would have given me quite an insight into that guy's character. (It all makes sense now...) Still, even just as a regular reading experience, this was quite enjoyable--the characters vivid, the British humor (in England they just call it "humor") often quite hilarious, the depiction of academia amusing and, I'm willing to bet, spot-on. While the aforementioned guy ultimately brought little to my life besides heartache, I suppose I can at least be grateful that he led me to <i>Lucky Jim</i>.</span>

          </span>

    <a title="Wayne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1113753-wayne"><img alt="Wayne" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44766821?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wayne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wayne" href="/user/show/1113753-wayne">Wayne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44766821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12956101156936721455">I didn't know much about this book, but had seen it on a few "best novels of the 20th century lists." I took it on a trip to Toronto with a few other lightweight books, and read it last. There were two key aspects about the book that hooked me. The first was the wonderful cast of very memorable and slightly crazy characters. Even the protagonist -- one Jim Dixon -- was host to several quirky characteristics. Yet the author managed to stay within the bounds of belief.<br/><br/>The second aspect was the wri</span>
  <span id="freeText12956101156936721455" style="display:none">I didn't know much about this book, but had seen it on a few "best novels of the 20th century lists." I took it on a trip to Toronto with a few other lightweight books, and read it last. There were two key aspects about the book that hooked me. The first was the wonderful cast of very memorable and slightly crazy characters. Even the protagonist -- one Jim Dixon -- was host to several quirky characteristics. Yet the author managed to stay within the bounds of belief.<br /><br />The second aspect was the writing: the author, Kingsley Amis, is a marvelous wordsmith and storyteller. Several times I went back and re-read sentences just for the sheer pleasure of reading the words again. And I found myself laughing out loud on many occasions.<br /><br />I can't recommend this book highly enough.</span>

<div id="review_87769354" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87769354" />
    <a title="rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2499261-rachel"><img alt="rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572660600p2/2499261.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87769354?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="rachel" href="/user/show/2499261-rachel">rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2499261-rachel?shelf=2010">2010</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer87769354&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87769354" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8876928826822298823">I loved the cleverness of this book and laughed out loud a few times, but I had too many problems to enjoy much else about it. <br/><br/>For one thing, I didn't find any of the characters compelling or worth caring about and often felt like I could put it down and be perfectly OK with not picking it back up again. <br/><br/>But mainly, I hated Dixon's attitude towards women. Specifically Margaret, who he clearly feels like he's settling for in the beginning and then who gets the shaft for Christine simply because s</span>
  <span id="freeText8876928826822298823" style="display:none">I loved the cleverness of this book and laughed out loud a few times, but I had too many problems to enjoy much else about it. <br /><br />For one thing, I didn't find any of the characters compelling or worth caring about and often felt like I could put it down and be perfectly OK with not picking it back up again. <br /><br />But mainly, I hated Dixon's attitude towards women. Specifically Margaret, who he clearly feels like he's settling for in the beginning and then who gets the shaft for Christine simply because she's...not as pretty? Personally, I wasn't invested in hoping for Dixon to get the best piece of arm candy possible. Blech. <br /><br />I <i>was</i>, however, interested in how Amis would reconcile Dixon's leaving a mentally unstable woman fresh off a suicide attempt for someone he's just more attracted to. It was disappointing that, instead of giving this any sort of reflection at all, Amis tosses in a <s>deus ex machina</s> second pill bottle and demonizes Margaret, absolving Dixon of any small guilt he had about the situation and leaving him to openly chase his pretty girl. LOL, what an unstable biatch that Margaret is!!!! That is supposed to be my reaction,right? It's a little insulting. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8876928826822298823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1310046784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1310046784" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1310046784?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1310046784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16767701234035404977">I tend to be very unfair to comic (humorous) novels, I have this unreasonable demand that every page makes me laugh. Quite a tall order for the poor authors I think, but I can’t help it, so I generally avoid reading comic novels. I stumbled upon an audiobook of <i>Lucky Jim</i> on Youtube and thought I’d give it a go as it is often listed as one of the all-time great novels; my aversion to comic novels notwithstanding (freebies conquer all). <br/><br/><i>Lucky Jim</i> is the story of James Dixon a history lecturer at a</span>
  <span id="freeText16767701234035404977" style="display:none">I tend to be very unfair to comic (humorous) novels, I have this unreasonable demand that every page makes me laugh. Quite a tall order for the poor authors I think, but I can’t help it, so I generally avoid reading comic novels. I stumbled upon an audiobook of <i>Lucky Jim</i> on Youtube and thought I’d give it a go as it is often listed as one of the all-time great novels; my aversion to comic novels notwithstanding (freebies conquer all). <br /><br /><i>Lucky Jim</i> is the story of James Dixon a history lecturer at a provincial English university. Dixon is not terribly good at his job, lacking interest in the subject he is teaching or academia itself. He is more interested in recruiting pretty students for his class. What he lacks in teaching skills he makes up for in sass. Throughout the novel Jim is preoccupied by Margaret, a fellow lecturer with emotional issues who he had a romantic relationship with. His attempt to break up with her triggers a hysteric reaction (possibly faux-hysteria as she is quite manipulative). He later meets and fall heads over heel in love with Christine Callaghan a more attractive girl with a good sense of humour, the only snag is she appears to be spoken for.<br /><br />There is not much more than that to the plot apart from Jim getting into scrapes and his attempt to get out of them by his outlandish plans which sometime backfire on him with hilarious results. His favorite artifice is to put on accents to make fake telephone calls to create an alibi for himself or to get out of appointments.<br /><br />Much of the humour of the book comes from the dialog and Jim’s unflattering observations of himself and other characters, and his unending sarcastic comments. <i>Lucky Jim</i> certainly did not make me laugh on every page but it is funny enough to get a pass from me. I feel there is not much substance to the book to treasure in the long term and the development of most characters tends to be rather perfunctory; especially female characters who are portrayed without much depth or agency. The only character the reader gets to know very well is Jim. An important aspect of the book’s humour is the satire of academic life from the faculty’s point of view, but I have too little knowledge or experience of such a life to appreciate this side of it.<br /><br />Anyway, I cannot think of much to say about this book, it worth reading if you like comic novels where the laughs come mainly from witticisms rather than slapsticks (though there is a bit of that too). I much prefer it to that other humorous classic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4921.Three_Men_in_a_Boat" title="Three Men in a Boat by Jerome K. Jerome" rel="nofollow noopener"> Three Men in a Boat</a> which I found to be almost entirely mirth-free. As for <i>Lucky Jim</i>’s status as a classic I have no idea what that is about.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16767701234035404977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=2017indchallenge">2017indchallenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=fun">fun</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2100438517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10239295883642121284">This is one of those books I've been meaning to read for years. The blurb I read ("funniest book of the 20th century", seemed hard to live up to. But it kept coming up on those lists of "have you read/must have read" etc. so I finally read it.<br/><br/>And it was amusing. Not the <i>funniest</i> book of the 20th century, maybe <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/310612.A_Confederacy_of_Dunces' title='A Confederacy of Dunces by John Kennedy Toole' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Confederacy of Dunces</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17094618.P__G__Wodehouse' title='P. G. Wodehouse' rel='nofollow noopener'>P. G. Wodehouse</a> or maybe even <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60063.Mapp_and_Lucia__Lucia___4_' title='Mapp and Lucia (Lucia, #4) by E.F. Benson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mapp and Lucia</a> holds that spot for me but funny in a Fawlty Towers kind of way. Very British, I think. I also think it must have be</span>
  <span id="freeText10239295883642121284" style="display:none">This is one of those books I've been meaning to read for years. The blurb I read ("funniest book of the 20th century", seemed hard to live up to. But it kept coming up on those lists of "have you read/must have read" etc. so I finally read it.<br /><br />And it was amusing. Not the <i>funniest</i> book of the 20th century, maybe <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/310612.A_Confederacy_of_Dunces" title="A Confederacy of Dunces by John Kennedy Toole" rel="nofollow noopener">A Confederacy of Dunces</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17094618.P__G__Wodehouse" title="P. G. Wodehouse" rel="nofollow noopener">P. G. Wodehouse</a> or maybe even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60063.Mapp_and_Lucia__Lucia___4_" title="Mapp and Lucia (Lucia, #4) by E.F. Benson" rel="nofollow noopener">Mapp and Lucia</a> holds that spot for me but funny in a Fawlty Towers kind of way. Very British, I think. I also think it must have been both more shocking and (therefore funnier) when it was published in the 1950s.<br /><br />Jim doesn't seem to be very lucky. He has a job as a college professor that he may or may not be able to keep (first year, no tenure, all very up in the air), he's involved with a woman he doesn't much like and soon is attracted to a woman (really more of a girl) who is already seeing someone (the son of his boss). His way of coping is to make faces. Really: I highlighted all the different faces Jim makes. It became funnier and funnier to me as the book went on.<br /><br />Jim is not terribly likable. He's lazy, he drinks too much, he doesn't seem to like people, he plays awful pranks. But I did find him funny and, by the end of the book, I did hope things would work out for him.<br /><br />A lot of the humor is in the nastiness of the people and their interactions. It's a kind of humor I have to admit to enjoying. People who are trying to be mean to others ending up looking like fools. And often the person in that situation is Jim.<br /><br />This book did not make me laugh out loud but it made me smile a lot. Very consistently amusing and highly enjoyable.<br /><br />And now I can check it off my list of "must read classics".</span>
  <a data-text-id="10239295883642121284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1866512866" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1866512866" />
    <a title="Cbj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5283293-cbj"><img alt="Cbj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579249711p2/5283293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1866512866?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cbj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cbj" href="/user/show/5283293-cbj">Cbj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=melancholic">melancholic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=tired-of-modern-life">tired-of-modern-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=alienation">alienation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=british-classics">british-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5283293-cbj?shelf=alcohol">alcohol</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1866512866" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15426210517714044364">I read John Osborne's play <b>Look Back in Anger</b> (1956) a few years ago. The play was very interesting to me because it conveyed the sense of anomie felt by the British as their empire which once seemed to be unassailable was now crumbling to pieces. There was a sense of despair and purposelessness in British society. I couldn't help but feel that even the idealistic Jimmy (<i>the angry leading man in the play</i>) was in some ways longing for that kind of world where people had a reason to live (<i>like bea</i></span>
  <span id="freeText15426210517714044364" style="display:none">I read John Osborne's play <b>Look Back in Anger</b> (1956) a few years ago. The play was very interesting to me because it conveyed the sense of anomie felt by the British as their empire which once seemed to be unassailable was now crumbling to pieces. There was a sense of despair and purposelessness in British society. I couldn't help but feel that even the idealistic Jimmy (<i>the angry leading man in the play</i>) was in some ways longing for that kind of world where people had a reason to live (<i>like beating the Nazis or even conquering other lands</i>). But Jimmy is disillusioned and angry with what he perceives as the lack of enthusiasm in post-world war two Britain. <br /><br />The self-hating Indian writer Nirad.C.Chaudhary made the following observation about British society in his book <b>A Passage to England</b>, which he wrote after a visit in 1955 - "<i>For individuals, as for nations; doing well in life and doing something in life are contradictory aims. The real test for the Welfare State will be whether it has been able to merge the two ends, so far as they can be merged. But it seems to me that this very important condition of the Welfare State's success is difficult of fulfillment in contemporary England. This difficulty is not due to an absence of men with a will to do something. The real trouble is that there is very little to do and it is very difficult to arrive at a clear perception of what to do. On this point, ever since the end of the war I have had a feeling that the English people are in the closing stages of one cycle of their existence and have not as yet entered on another.</i>"<br /><br /><b>Lucky Jim</b> was written a few years before Look Back in Anger and A Passage to England came out. In Lucky Jim, Jim Dixon is involved in a lowly temporary job at a British university. Life is tough. His job involves trying to please the mediocre Mr.Welch, a senior professor who has the power to make Jim's position permanent, staving off the over-enthusiastic and bossy student Michie and waiting patiently for his paper to be published by a plagiarizing journal editor. His social life is also nothing to write home about. It involves attending singing weekends at Mr.Welch's house, suffering Mr.Welch's aggressive son Bernard who is an aspiring painter, trying to hide his attraction towards Bertrand's beautiful girlfriend Christine and also placating the neurotic and suicidal Margaret, who Jim feels is the woman he truly deserves. It is a life of self-repression and disappointment. The only relief is the copious amounts of alcohol and the rationed cigarettes that Jim consumes regularly. The novel has quite a few instances of Amis describing Jim's inner thoughts and how he tries to mask them by assuming ridiculous expressions on his face.<br /><br />Jim is a bit like Jimmy, the angry protagonist in John Osborne's play and Nirad.C.Chaudhary might have been writing about people like Jim, in A Passage to England. Jim is an ex-RAF corporal and he finds it tough to fit in with the university crowd with its refined tastes and strict manners. He takes up the temporary job at the University because he did not think he would be any use as a school teacher. There seemed to be nothing else for him to do. I think Jim represents British youth who is inspired by American pop culture and consumerism (<i>there is a bit in the novel where Jim expresses his preference for pop songs over Classical music that is preferred by the university crowd</i>) which is at odds with the likes of Mr.Welch who represent the Old England. <br /><br />Lucky Jim is a bittersweet novel filled with understated humor (<i>it has one of the best descriptions of a hangover - His mouth had been used as a latrine by some small creature of the night, and then as its mausoleum.</i>). I wouldn't be surprised if Ricky Gervais and Stephen Merchant were inspired by the novel when they wrote <b>The Office</b> (UK). Afterall, David Brent, Tim and Gareth exhibit many of the same maladies of the characters in Lucky Jim.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15426210517714044364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText328382697395166642" style="display:none">I wasn't expecting to enjoy this, but I really, really, did. I found it funny, too.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_367040985" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/367040985" />
    <a title="David Lentz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz"><img alt="David Lentz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473090881p2/3604115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/367040985?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lentz" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz">David Lentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer367040985" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5595630676548684502">Jim Dixon is a testament to God's grace on this earth: he is genuinely a horrible man in nearly every respect and yet, call it grace or luck, he plays well with random chance and it generates a few good laughs. Lucky Jim is completely unworthy of all the grace which seems to come his way. Consider that he is reckless, disloyal, drunk, a fool, an idiot with women, sexist, clumsy and self-destructive beyond belief. He is the anti-hero in the tradition of Martin Amis and every JP Donleavy protagoni</span>
  <span id="freeText5595630676548684502" style="display:none">Jim Dixon is a testament to God's grace on this earth: he is genuinely a horrible man in nearly every respect and yet, call it grace or luck, he plays well with random chance and it generates a few good laughs. Lucky Jim is completely unworthy of all the grace which seems to come his way. Consider that he is reckless, disloyal, drunk, a fool, an idiot with women, sexist, clumsy and self-destructive beyond belief. He is the anti-hero in the tradition of Martin Amis and every JP Donleavy protagonist you may ever know. But lucky for Jim, grace isn't earned. Otherwise, grace would place a divine debt upon the Almighty for human good behavior and grace, indeed, seems to come so liberally to those least worthy of it. But grace is the only hope for redemption for some who can't save us from ourselves. He makes virtually every error in interpersonal relations that any man can make. But this propensity to play the fool connects the reader to Jim and he becomes a sort of high-brow, campus Everyman. Like Donleavy's wild, witty Ginger Man, only the author ever really knows why Lucky Jim behaves so outrageously. But in Jim's case his shabby conduct seems to be connected to a bundle of frustration, ineptitude, rage and lack of self-esteem. My purpose is not to analyze the flaws of Jim as a proxy for the author himself but rather to understand why this literary figure is comic and due to his pure luck, therefore, not concurrently tragicomic. Amis could easily have made this story line a tragedy were it not for the intervention of hap. But it's the hap which brings happiness to the reader in a satisfying story line which, taken in another direction, would be ever so much less so. Amis wants you to see Jim as an absurd figure, which as a comic anti-hero he certainly is and serves as an early archetype in the modern novel of many absurd and bewildered men who followed Jim later well into the PoMo movement after World War II. Of course, the novel seems terribly sexist today or is this figure just an honest rendition of the way men really are and have been since the days of Homer's Circe? That is, how many times have men been cast into the role of swine before the loveliness and power of women of sheer, naked pulchritude. It seems that, alas, Jim wants to be with both an attractive, sweet woman as well as with one of intellect and perception. But Jim is torn between an either/or to find intellectual depth in pure beauty and beauty in pure intellect. Jim strikes one as far more shallow than he is lucky. But that's the way that grace works, isn't it? That is, the unworthy seem to gain an unfair share of grace, at least, in the short term and you usually need to wait outside the scope of the time frame to see the equilibrium come into play with redemption or punishment or both. It has occurred to me that in this wildly random world, what we call luck is simply a short-term gain awaiting an offsetting long-term loss, which results ultimately over time in balance or sophrosyne. Maybe this is simply how the spiritual power of the universe imposes what we know as some semblance of justice. Lucky for Jim that grace is not earned because his behavior seems so unworthy of it. But who am I to judge the fortunes of others? The question arose in my mind as to whether Lucky Jim's fortune is more good luck or bad luck. Further, one wonders whether with all the good fortune, Amis created Jim's many boons with too much artifice. Is this narrative just all too good to be true? Certainly, Jim is the personal agent of nearly every odd misfortune to complicate his life and his bad karma is off the charts.<br />Yet he doesn't seem personally to drive much of the good fortune which befalls him because of his radical social ineptitude. I wanted to dislike Jim immensely because he behaved so offensively until I found myself in him and then I began to find that hope stirred in my soul for Lucky Jim. In the end I wanted him to find that somehow he had earned the upside of the cycle of his fortune. And while he may have remained unredeemed, except for the redemption that love brings, it is the magic of the narrative of Amis which enabled me to see Jim's all-too-human side with a recognition that somehow but for the grace of God, for better or worse, any of us could be some version of Lucky Jim. And, in fact, many of us probably were. Because Amis gives us such an unsavory character whom ultimately it is possible to care for by virtue of the narrative and the comedy, this protagonist somehow manages to instill the most odd willing suspension of disbelief. I give fair warning that patience is required at the inception of this novel: it just seems impossibly high-brow and stuffy and perhaps even cute as so called campus humor of the sort of which the blurber for this Penguin edition, David Lodge, wrote in his novels. But, once again, it is the high civility of the British as a civilization which becomes the straight man and source of so much their most endearing and hilarious comedy. This book just barely crossed over into five-star territory but, luckily, to be fair there's just too much writing talent here in Amis to do otherwise gracefully.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5595630676548684502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_367040985'><a id="like_count_review_367040985" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/367040985?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">10 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F395182.Lucky_Jim">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_2485813749" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2485813749" />
    <a title="Jay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6137835-jay"><img alt="Jay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521161690p2/6137835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2485813749?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jay" href="/user/show/6137835-jay">Jay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6137835-jay?shelf=it-s-about-damned-time">it-s-about-damned-time</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2485813749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4946988852978131056">Dixon’s (the protagonist’s) whiskey- and sherry-fueled delivery of his “Merrie Olde England” lecture to the whole college had me in tears - of laughter and earned a “star” on its own merits. A departing “beau geste” on a par with that of David Warner, hurtling through London on a motorcycle, wearing a burning gorilla costume, near the end of the film “Morgan!” Viva the anti-hero! <br/>More later...<br/><br/>OK, here’s the “More”: One of the “bonuses” of this novel is Amis’s rich *English* English and vocabular</span>
  <span id="freeText4946988852978131056" style="display:none">Dixon’s (the protagonist’s) whiskey- and sherry-fueled delivery of his “Merrie Olde England” lecture to the whole college had me in tears - of laughter and earned a “star” on its own merits. A departing “beau geste” on a par with that of David Warner, hurtling through London on a motorcycle, wearing a burning gorilla costume, near the end of the film “Morgan!” Viva the anti-hero! <br />More later...<br /><br />OK, here’s the “More”: One of the “bonuses” of this novel is Amis’s rich *English* English and vocabulary that seems to have disappeared from most writing, let alone conversation, today. Examples: “objurgatory”; “fulminant”; “pomatum”; “allotrope”; “ataxically”; “deliquescence”; “havering” [sic] as in Dixon’s silent insult of Dr. Welch “You havering, slavering get”. Only an Englishman could muster such a cut. And then “untowardly”, “censoriously”, and let’s throw in “Horla”* for dessert. For you music lovers: “maestoso” and my particular favorite - “allegro con fuoco”, a tempo I believe was created for accordion wanna-be’s. Then, of course, the descriptions of characters: “whey-faced” and Dr. Welch’s nose as “a large, open-pored tetrahedron”. None of these (and there are many others I could cite) is out of place in his writing, but, rather, emerge from the quotidian with an easy grace and elegance reminiscent of Willy Mays’ catching a fly ball over his shoulder on a dead run.<br /><br />*As in de Maupassant’s short story “The Horla” - one of the scariest and most disturbing stories I ever read as a pre-teen. Here, Amis uses it in “a tobacco-loving Horla” as Our Hero struggles through a hangover to explain why his bedding seems to have a large hole burned through them that wasn’t there when he passed out. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4946988852978131056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Rgy2UqcnWdzdrV+PWZT3rOd4ouFdIX4DvGDcZ/RwgSpOB5oWkAhL4c0lk4dWoQOtx6rnk9I8OoaEJjXnM10xOw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VnzhFw94IKXGER/HEugMRn5kk1jR4NYhmjENZty8goBed81TOFcymNaZ088d3fhHXrbWKl79kqSid+TmG5EykQ==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer883721399126959270">Sir Kingsley William Amis, CBE, was an English novelist, poet, critic, and teacher. He wrote more than twenty novels, three collections of poetry, short stories, radio and television scripts, and books of social and literary criticism. He fathered the English novelist <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11337.Martin_Amis' title='Martin Amis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Martin Amis</a>.<br/><br/>Kingsley Amis was born in Clapham, Wandsworth, County of London (now South London), England, the son of William Robert </span>
  <span id="freeText883721399126959270" style="display:none">Sir Kingsley William Amis, CBE, was an English novelist, poet, critic, and teacher. He wrote more than twenty novels, three collections of poetry, short stories, radio and television scripts, and books of social and literary criticism. He fathered the English novelist <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11337.Martin_Amis" title="Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Martin Amis</a>.<br /><br />Kingsley Amis was born in Clapham, Wandsworth, County of London (now South London), England, the son of William Robert Amis, a mustard manufacturer's clerk. He began his education at the City of London School, and went up to St. John's College, Oxford April 1941 to read English; it was there that he met Philip Larkin, with whom he formed the most important friendship of his life. After only a year, he was called up for Army service in July 1942. After serving as a lieutenant in the Royal Corps of Signals in the Second World War, Amis returned to Oxford in October 1945 to complete his degree. Although he worked hard and got a first in English in 1947, he had by then decided to give much of his time to writing.<br /><br />Pen names: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/553548.Robert_Markham" rel="nofollow noopener">Robert Markham</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4300334.William_Bill_Tanner" rel="nofollow noopener">William Tanner</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="883721399126959270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/876732-lucky-jim">Quotes from Lucky Jim</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;If you can't annoy somebody, there is little point in writing.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/10470-if-you-can-t-annoy-somebody-there-is-little-point-in">1365 likes</a>

            <div class="clear"></div>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Dixon was alive again. Consciousness was upon him before he could get out of the way; not for him the slow, gracious wandering from the halls of sleep, but a summary, forcible ejection. He lay sprawled, too wicked to move, spewed up like a broken spider-crab on the tarry shingle of morning. The light did him harm, but not as much as looking at things did; he resolved, having done it once, never to move his eyeballs again. A dusty thudding in his head made the scene before him beat like a pulse. His mouth had been used as a latrine by some small creature of the night, and then as its mausoleum. During the night, too, he'd somehow been on a cross-country run and then been expertly beaten up by secret police. He felt bad.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/47067-dixon-was-alive-again-consciousness-was-upon-him-before-he">74 likes</a>
